                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Aqueous+Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_aqueous%20sodium-ion%20battery">aqueous sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na0.44mno2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na0.44MnO2">Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tunnel+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_tunnel%20structure">tunnel structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_doping">doping</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In energy, the whole world is trying to switch from fossil fuel to renewable clean energies such as solar, tidal, and wind powers, which however heavily depend on the development of a grade-scale energy storage system (ESS) to smooth the undulate the output of these energy forms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> In the mechanical and electrochemical ESS, rechargeable batteries, with high energy converting ratio, long-period life, and nonpollution running, are considered to be of prodigious long-term potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Lithium-ion battery (LIB) is the most popular at the moment because of its high energy and longevity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> However, the scarce lithium supplies and potential risk related to safety in LIB led to consideration of other type of batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Aqueous rechargeable sodium-ion battery (ARSIB) shows a bright prospect in ESS, ascribed to the wide distribution sodium reserves, nonflammable aqueous electrolyte, and environmental protection. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 9 to 13">(9−13)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical characteristic of ARSIB depends largely on the electrode materials, especially the cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14−16)</a> Various cathode materials have been investigated to achieve high-energy and long-cycle ARSIB, such as manganese-based oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 17 to 24">(17−24)</a> Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 9 and 25 to 29">(9,25−29)</a> and polyanion compounds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 30 to 35">(30−35)</a> Among them, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has been intensively explored as a promising candidate for ARSIB, due to its low price, high capacity (120 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and unique tunnel-type structure, which is of benefit to sodiation/desodiation and chemical/electrochemical steadiness within aqueous electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> However, the working potential range of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is from −0.8 to 0.8 V versus SHE (contributions to the crystallographic transformations in the Na<sub>0.66–0.22</sub> regions), with half capacity under 0 V versus SHE. The Na-rich phase with Na content higher than 0.44 is a thermodynamic instability material in air and hard to be prepared through a general high-temperature reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> It leads to Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> usually showing a specific capacity of no more than 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and low energy density in aqueous electrolytes (no more than 50 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 17, 39 and 40">(17,39,40)</a> Recently, a high capacity of 101 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained by the presodium method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The frustrating thing is that half of the reversible capacity functions below 0 V versus SHE, with very little increase to the energy density of the full cell. If the Na-rich phase with high working potential can be successfully produced under ambient conditions, the energy density of a full cell based on the Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode should be dramatically improved. Hu and co-workers developed air-stable Fe-doped Na<sub>0.61</sub>[Mn<sub>0.61–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ti<sub>0.39</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> positive electrodes with 0.61 Na content in the tunnel structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The usable capacity of it is approximately 90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with 3.4 V average working voltage versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in an organic electrolyte. However, a stable Na-richer Na<sub>0.66</sub> phase with higher capacity is hardly reported, especially in aqueous electrolytes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we develop a stable Na-rich Fe-based tunnel structure Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode material for ARSIB through a simple solid state reaction under ambient conditions. It exhibits the high discharge capacity of 95 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.75 V high average potential in the voltage range −0.3 to 1.3 V versus SHE. Contrary to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> during the desodiation/sodiation process, the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> redox couple contributes to approximately half of the charge compensation of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> leading to the high working potential, confirmed by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and the Mössbauer spectrum. The large ionic radius of Sn doping helps maintain the tunnel structure, suppressing the phase transitions to a layered structure brought from the Fe doping. And the Sn 4d octahedron contributes greatly to the conduction band of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, which benefits its conductivity, facilitating the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Benefiting from the synergistic effect of Fe and Sn doping, Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> not only delivers a high specific capacity at high working potential but also presents a long cycle life and high rate capability. The battery assembled with the NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C anode exhibits a high energy density up to 80 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> (on account of both electrodes’ active materials), with the working voltage of 1.5 V and a high capacity retention of 84% after 1000 cycles at 1 C.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Methods and Materials</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p">Tunnel structure Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> was prepared through a solid-state reaction between MnCO<sub>3</sub> (AR., Sigma-Aldrich) and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (AR., Sigma-Aldrich). Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> was also synthesized by a solid–state reaction using MnCO<sub>3</sub> (AR., Sigma-Aldrich), Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (100 nm, AR., Sigma-Aldrich), SnO<sub>2</sub> (50 nm, AR., Sigma-Aldrich), TiO<sub>2</sub> (50 nm, AR., Sigma-Aldrich), and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (AR., Sigma-Aldrich) by a stoichiometric ratio. Five mol % excess Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was added to compensate for the Na loss. After ball milling and pelleting, pellets were given heat treatment. First, they were heated to 500 °C with 5 °C/min standing for 5 h and then increased to 950 °C with 5 °C/min standing for 10 h. After the mixture was cooled to room temperature naturally and ground, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> were obtained.</div><div class="NLM_p last">NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C was obtained by a high-temperature reaction at 750 °C for 5 h. The precursors were sodium tartrate (99%), NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (99%), and H<sub>2</sub>TiO<sub>3</sub> (85%). First, the three powders were ground and mixed with the stoichiometric ratio in water. Then, the sample was spray dried. The powder was heated at 750 °C for 5 h in N<sub>2</sub> (99.9%). The as-prepared NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C was obtained after cooling down naturally.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Philips X’pert X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54182 Å) was used to detect the structure of the as-prepared samples at a scan rate of 0.008842° s<sup>–1</sup>. Powder profile refinement program GSAS was used to refine the crystal structures for Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (0.26 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.63). Field-emitting scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, JEOL-JSM-6700F) was used to detect the microstructure of the products. A JEOL ARM200CF microscope at 200 kV was applied to obtain transmission electron microscopy and energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometry (EDS) elemental mapping images. Inductive coupled plasma-atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES, Optima 7300DV) was used to detect the content of Na, Mn, Fe, Ti, and Sn. A high-resolution Kratos AXIS 165 X-ray photoelectron spectrometer was used to detect the value of elements. A JEM-ARM200F STEM fitted instrument was used to measure high-angle annular-dark field (HAADF) images and Ti L<sub>2,3</sub>, Mn L<sub>2,3</sub>, Fe L<sub>2,3</sub>, and Sn M edges electron energy loss spectrum (EELS). A customized cell with an open aperture was used for the in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) experiment during charging/discharging. The in situ battery was cycled at 0.2 C. BL14W1 of the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF, China) in transmission mode at beamline was used to obtain ex situ XAS data for Mn and Fe <i>K</i>-edge. A Topologic System Incorporated spectrometer, using <sup>57</sup>Co γ-ray source, was used to obtain Mössbauer results calibrated by α-Fe at room temperature. MossWinn 3.0 software was used for fitting. The full cells after cycling were disassembled to test the electrodes. Before testing, the electrodes were washed with ethanol three times and vacuum-dried for 12 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The cathode was obtained by mixing the active material, poly(vinylidene fluoride) and super P using nitromethyl pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent at a mass ratio of 8:1:1. The formed viscous slurry is easily coated onto the Ti mesh (100 mesh). The anodes were prepared similarly. By mixing the anode active material, poly(vinylidene fluoride), and super P in NMP with mass ratio of 8:1:1, the slurry was smeared onto a tin-coated copper screen (100 mesh). After being dried at 100 °C in a vacuum oven, the electrodes were rolled under 5 MPa. The loadings of the both cathode and anode were approximately 10 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Three-electrode systems with Pt and HgO/Hg electrodes were used to test the electrochemical properties at room temperature. Full batteries with a nonwoven separator were assembled in a glovebox with Ar gas. A land cycler (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Corporation) was used to test the cycle stability. Before testing, the cells were left standing for 10 h. CHI 600E electrochemical workstations were used to obtain cyclic voltammograms. The potentials of electrodes were transformed to the SHE for the sake of convenience.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Diffusion Barrier-Energy Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To find out the sodium diffusion energetics and kinetics within tunnel-type Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculation was performed. DFT calculations were performed using the DMol3 software package using the Materials Studio software. The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof functional, a component of general gradient approximation, was performed to depict exchange-dependent interactions. Partial density of states (pDOS) calculations were conducted utilizing a Quintet numerical polarization basis set. The semiempirical Tkatchenko–Scheffler scheme was incorporated to illustrate van der Waals interactions. DFT semicore pseudopotentials were executed to account for the core electrons. The Brillouin zone was sampled at the Γ point using a Monkhorst–Pack grid for all systems. The self-consistent field convergence criterion for electronic energy was set to 1.0 × 1 × 10<sup>–6</sup> hartree. The convergence thresholds of diffusion energy and max forces/displacement for geometry optimization were 1.0 × 10<sup>–5</sup> hartree, 0.002 hartree/Å, and 0.005 Å, respectively. To analyze the sodium ion migration pathways, linear and quadratic synchronous transit methods were employed in conjunction with conjugated gradient refinement.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Structure of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> was synthesized through a simple high-temperature solid-state reaction under an air atmosphere. It can be observed from the XRD pattern that the diffraction peaks were obtained from the orthorhombic crystal system (up curve in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a), without obvious impurity phases. The Rietveld refinements of XRD are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. The <i>Pbam</i> space group can be indexed for the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> sample, revealing it is isostructural with the typical tunnel-type of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The framework of as-prepared Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> is built up based on edge-sharing MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra double and triple chains and corner-sharing MO<sub>5</sub> cubic pyramid single chains, forming S-shaped and smaller pentagon tunnels. In large S-shaped tunnels, there are two sodium sites [Na(2) and Na(3)]. The smaller pentagon tunnel loads with another site [Na(1)]. The refinement showed that the Na(1) site is fully occupied (100%). The occupations of Na3 and Na2 sites are 98.5% and 100%, respectively (schematic in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). It gives a total Na content calculated of ∼0.65, which is higher than the occupancy in Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1 and Table S2</a>). We take the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> as a nominal composition close to the inductively coupled plasma results (Na<sub>0.665</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>). Compared with Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, the refined lattice parameters of three axes are enlarged than that of Na<sub>0.665</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). And the refined results show that the Mn sites were randomly substituted by Fe, Ti, and Sn elements. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra were obtained to detect the elemental composition and valence state of the elements. The full range survey and Mn, Fe, Ti, Sn scans of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> reveal the existing of the Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, and Sn<sup>4+</sup> ions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d–h). The oxidation state of manganese in Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> is lower than that of undoped materials, suggesting the successful doping of Fe, Ti, and Sn ions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). The as-prepared particles are rod-like with a diameter of around 1 μm and a length of about 5 μm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i,j), which are similar to that of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). It can be seen that Mn, Fe, Ti, and Sn are uniformly distributed, according to the EDS elemental mapping image. It indicates uniform doping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>k). The crystal lattices of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> are in good agreement with tunnel-type structure according to the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>l). The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) results reconfirm its orthorhombic lattice symmetry.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/medium/am4c18772_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural characterization of the Fe-based tunnel-type oxide. (a) XRD patterns of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. (b) Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (c) Crystal structure of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. XPS full range survey (d) and Mn (e), Fe (f), Ti (g), Sn (h) scans of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (i) SEM images of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (j) TEM image and corresponding EDS images (k) of Mn, Fe, Ti, and Sn elements. (l) HR-TEM and SAED images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c18772&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further study the atomic structure, spherical aberration-corrected scanning TEM (STEM) with a HAADF was performed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b show the HAADF images of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> along the [001] zone axis. The bright dots representing the heavy atomic columns (Mn, Fe, Ti, and Sn) are lined up in a regular pattern to form the typical large S-shaped tunnel and small hexagonal tunnel fully filled with light Na atoms. The five different transition-metal sites are evident and distinguishable. Three identifiable different Na sites were also clearly observed: two Na ions within S-shaped tunnel and one Na ion within the hexagonal tunnel. Atomic-scale STEM-electron energy loss spectrum (EELS) experiments were conducted to further discriminate four transition metal ions’ distribution preferences and their valence states. Line scanning EELS and the Ti, Sn, Mn, and Fe EELS spectra within the selected yellow area were both collected. The bright dots shown on the projection view of the stacked EELS spectra represent the excitation of the EELS signal for the corresponding elements therein, while the contrast represents its relative content. Mn is mainly the Mn<sup>3+</sup> state, in accordance with the XPS results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). Ti, Fe, and Sn are typically the Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and Sn<sup>4+</sup> states, respectively. It can be deduced that the Ti, Sn, Mn, and Fe are randomly and uniformly distributed on all transition-metal sites. To further figure out the element distribution, atomic-scale EDS was also performed. It can be observed that Ti, Sn, Mn, and Fe locate at five transition-metal sites without a preference (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c–f). These results are well in agreement with refinement calculations. The disordered Ti, Sn, Mn, and Fe arrangement determines the distinct electrochemical performances of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/medium/am4c18772_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Atomic structure. (a) HAADF-STEM and EELS images of the as-prepared Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> materials along the [001] zone axis. (b) HAADF-STEM image. (c–f) Matched Fe, Mn, Ti, and Sn elemental distribution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c18772&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">For further investigation of this material character, different relevant Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.66–<i>x</i></sub>FexTi<sub>0.34–<i>y</i></sub>Sn<sub><i>y</i></sub>]O<sub>2</sub> compounds with different doping ratios were prepared. The phase-pure Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.44</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> with low Fe content was successfully obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, Supporting Information). Phase-pure Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.44</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.34</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> failed to synthesize. The attempt to obtain Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.34</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> similarly failed. The corresponding sample showed a mixture of tunnel-type and layered structures (<i>P</i>2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> marked with an asterisk) as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, Supporting Information. These phenomena reconcile the results of previous reported work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 and 41">(38,41)</a> When the Na content in the tunnels is too high, the strong repulsive force between them leads to large lattice strains. It is also difficult to synthesize phase-pure Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.44</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, Supporting Information). The hard structural strain should be blamed. The big radius Sn<sup>4+</sup> doping may perform as a hinge to relieve lattice strains, with the cell parameters of three axes and lattice volume (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) expanded. With the Sn<sup>4+</sup> ratio up to 0.05, the phase transitions from a tunnel to layer structure happened again (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that an appropriate amount of Sn<sup>4+</sup> is essential to stabilize the tunnel structure.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Sodium Storage Property</h3><div class="NLM_p">The sodiation/desodiation character of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> was evaluated using a three-electrode system with the voltage from −0.3 to 1.3 V versus SHE. In our previous work, we found that H<sup>+</sup> would coinsert into the tunnel structure with Na<sup>+</sup> during the discharging process below 0 V versus SHE. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> The concentrated electrolyte 17 M (mol L<sup>–1</sup>) NaClO<sub>4</sub> was used as the electrolyte to solve this issue. There are three consecutive pairs of redox peaks in the CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>: reductive peaks at −0.2, 0.1, and 1.1 V and oxidative peaks at −0.15, 0.16, and 1.15 V versus SHE, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). This differs from the unsubstituted Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, which exhibits four consecutive redox pairs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, Supporting Information). The constant current charge–discharge curves exhibit a similar redox process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). There are three voltage platforms observed. One single plateau is located at potential from 0.8 to 1.3 V and one sloping profile is located at the middle potential (0–0.5 V). The third plateau is located at a potential from −0.3 to 0 V. It might result from the ordering breaking of charge and/or Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy, owing to the chaotic distribution of Fe, Ti, Mn, and Sn elements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> At 0.1 C, it delivers the initial charging/discharging capacity of 103/95 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a high average working potential of 0.75 V versus SHE, corresponding to an exchange of 0.34 Na<sup>+</sup> per molecular unit. However, at 0.1 C, Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.44</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> gives a low specific capacity of 60 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> shows a discharge capacity of 60 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 C, suggesting its good rate capability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/medium/am4c18772_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of this Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (a) CV profiles. (b) Constant current charging/discharging curves at 0.1 C (1 C corresponding to 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (c) Rate performances. (d) Charging/discharging curves of the NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C anode, the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode, and the ARSIB system consisting of the two electrodes. (e) Cycling behavior of the full cell. (f) Electrochemical performances of various ARSIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c18772&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Coupling with the NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C anode (the XRD and morphology are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, Supporting Information), the full battery was assembled under a cathode/anode mass ratio of 0.78 with an anode capacity 1% higher than that of the cathode (the charge/discharge performances of the anode are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, Supporting Information). It shows a discharge capacity of 95 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the cathode) with voltage from 0 to 2.0 V at 0.1 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). Three serial plateaus located at approximately 1.7, 1.0, and 0.5 V can be observed. The average working potential is 1.5 V. A maximum mass energy density of 80 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> is obtained. The total mass of active materials is studied to evaluate battery energy density (calculation details in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> Note S1). On the contrary, the full cell Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode gives the low discharge capacity of ∼45 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Its energy density (39 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>) cannot be compared to that of the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>/NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C battery (Note S1). Even at 2 C, the as-assembled Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>/NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C full battery could deliver a high discharge capacity of 60 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (∼63% of 0.1 C), suggesting good rate capability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). After 1000 cycles, a high capacity retention of 80% is obtained, demonstrating the longevity. The long cycle stability is also achieved at a low rate of 0.2 C. After 40 cycles, the battery exhibits 90% capacity retention, indicating the high stable structure of the cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). Moreover, it can output a high discharge capacity of 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under a low temperature of −10 °C at a 2 C rate, demonstrating an acceptable low-temperature electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). On the contrary, after 100 cycles, the full cell using a 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte only exhibits a capacity retention of 60% at 1 C, revealing that there should be serious water splitting on the electrode during cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Compared with previous reported ARSIBs, the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>/NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C full cell exhibits the highest energy density value. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref18 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref32 ref39 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref18 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref32 ref39 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 13, 18, 26 to 28, 32, 39 and 43 to 46">(13,18,26−28,32,39,43−46)</a></div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.3.  Structural Evolution and Charge Compensation</h3><div class="NLM_p">To explore the structural evolution of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> during the charge/discharge process, in situ XRD experiments within the initial charge/discharge process between −0.3 and 1.3 V versus SHE were carried out as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. During the initial charge process, XRD peaks located at 16.6° (140), 32.3° (340), 35.5° (360), 36.6° (151), and (140) show continuous high-angle changing along with desodiation. When it was discharged to −0.3 V, these peaks shift back close to their initial positions. Except for slight peak intensity changes, on obvious new phase was found. The tunnel structure should be maintained and kept stable. According to in situ XRD data Rietveld refinement results, the unit cell parameter changes of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> at different potentials were determined. During the charge process, the <i>a</i>-axis length is decreased from 9.238 to 9.076 Å with 1.75% reduction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). It gets back in position during the discharge process. The b- and <i>c</i>-axes exhibit a similar variation trend. The XRD patterns of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode electrodes using the 17 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte before and after 40 cycles at 0.2 C show that the main diffraction peaks are almost unchanged, demonstrating its structure stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). The SEM image also reveals the well-kept rod-like morphology and integrity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). The results presented above suggest the high structural reversibility of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. As Ti and Sn ions will remain at Ti<sup>4+</sup> and Sn<sup>4+</sup> during the charging process, the Mn and Fe valence states should be variable to keep charge neutrality. To determine the oxidation state variations of Mn and Fe during charging/discharging, <i>K</i>-edge XAS spectra were performed. The X-ray absorption near edge spectroscopy (XANES) of Fe shows an entire edge shift, which represents a chemical valence change of Fe ions during the charge and discharge process. As it charged to 1.3 V, the Fe <i>K</i>-edge turns to higher energy, indicating Fe<sup>4+</sup> formation by oxidization of Fe<sup>3+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). In addition, below 0.6 V, the Fe <i>K</i>-edge shows little shift. The relatively significant shift is observed above 1.0 V, which suggests that the oxidation of Fe<sup>3+</sup> should mainly proceed at a high potential. During discharging from 1.3 to −0.3 V, the <i>K</i>-edge curves of Fe shift to low energy, indicating the reduction of Fe<sup>4+</sup>. The prominent peaks at 1.72 Å in the FT k3-weighted extended XAS spectra of Fe should be corresponding to the Fe–O bond (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). As the battery is charging, the Fe–O bond becomes shorter. After discharging, it extends close to the original length, suggesting the strong Fe–O bond. During charging, the edge energy evolution of the Mn XANES results reveal that the valence of Mn ions shifts from +3 to +4. During discharging, the oxidation state of Mn turns to +3 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e). In contrast to Fe, below 0.6 V, the significant shift of the Mn <i>K</i>-edge is observed below 1.0 V, which suggests that the oxidation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> should mainly proceed at low potential. The Mn–O bond also exhibits a stretching vibration along with charging/discharging (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). Both significant changes indicate that both Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> are engaged in charge-transfer processes during cycling. Meanwhile, the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions exhibit good reversibility. <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectroscopy for pristine and electrodes at different voltages charged in the three-electrode system was carried out to further distinguish the valence change of Fe ions. The Mössbauer spectrum of the pristine powder is fitted to a bimodal distribution, belonging to Fe<sup>3+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g). According to the computational multipeak resolution method, the fitting data show that the ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup> with a coordination number of 6 and lying in the octahedral site is approximately 0.667. The ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup> that lay in the square pyramid with coordination number 5 is ∼0.334 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, Supporting Information). It suggests that Fe ion doping is random and uniform, agreeing with the EELS results. With the electrode charged to 0.6 V, almost no Fe<sup>3+</sup> is oxidized (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>, Supporting Information), which is consistent with electrochemical performances and XANES of Fe discussed above. With the potential up to 1.0 V, the spectrum is fitted to four doublet components, assigned to Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>4+</sup>, with 0.296 Fe<sup>3+</sup> located at the octahedral site (coordination number 6), 0.257 Fe<sup>3+</sup> located at the square pyramid (coordination number 5), 0.395 Fe<sup>4+</sup> lay in the octahedral site (coordination number 6), and 0.052 Fe<sup>4+</sup> lay in the square pyramid (coordination number 5) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>, Supporting Information). The intensity ratios of Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> are about 44.7 to 55.3, suggesting that about half of Fe<sup>3+</sup> was oxidized. After charging to 1.2 V, there is about 0.292 Fe<sup>3+</sup> located at the octahedral site (coordination number 6), 0.044 Fe<sup>3+</sup> located at the square pyramid (coordination number 5), 0.599 Fe<sup>4+</sup> located at the octahedral site (coordination number 6), and 0.065 Fe<sup>4+</sup> located at the square pyramid (coordination number 5) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>, Supporting Information). The intensity ratios of Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> are approximately 66.4 to 3.6, indicating that roughly 66.4% ofconstructional Fe atoms are engaged in the charge compensation during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. The high potential Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> redox leads to the high working potential of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, which contributes the majority of the energy density.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/medium/am4c18772_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Structural evolution and charge compensation mechanism of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (a,b) In situ XRD patterns for first cycle and corresponding cell parameter change. (c) Ex situ XANES at Fe <i>K</i>-edge. (d) Radial distribution around the iron ion. (e) Ex situ XANES at the Mn <i>K</i>-edge. (f) Radial distribution around the Mn ion. <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectra of the pristine Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> sample (g). Electrodes charged to 0.6 V (h), 1.0 V (i), and 1.2 V (j).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c18772&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Further understanding of the desodiation process and charge compensation was illustrated by theoretical calculations. The energy barriers of three different Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths were simulated to determine plausible Na-storage reactions pathways: Na(1) to Na(1) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), Na(2)–Na(2) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b), and Na(3)–Na(3) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). The diffusion barrier from Na(1) to Na(1) of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> is slightly lower, suggesting that Fe, Ti, and Sn doping contributes to fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d). Similarly, the diffusion barriers of Na(2) to Na(2) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e) and Na(3) to Na(3) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f) show faster diffusion kinetics for Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, the above results reveal that the Na(1)–Na(1) path is slower than that of Na(2) to Na(2) and Na(3) to Na(3), consistent with previous reports<sup>[</sup> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref25" aria-label="reference 12 and 25">(12,25)</a> The fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics result in good rate performance and cycle stability of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> as discussed above. To determine the charge compensation during charging, the electronic properties of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> were evaluated by simulated pDOS. The DOS plots reveal the semiconducting character of the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> samples. The Mn 3d orbital contributes the majority to the valence band below the Fermi level (<i>E</i><sub>f</sub>), demonstrating Mn accounts for the charge compensation during electrochemical desodiation/sodiation of the Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g,h). The Fe 3d orbital, in contrast, contributes more to the valence band below the <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> than the Mn 3d and O 2p states, confirming the charge compensation from Fe during charging/discharging (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i,j). Besides, the Sn 4d orbital contributes significantly to the conduction band of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. It is conducive to its electron conductibility, which leads to good rate capability and cycle stability. After 40 cycles at 0.2 C, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy tests of the Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>/NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C battery using the 1–3–2 Na electrolyte reveal a little increase of the semicircle diameter compared with that of pristine, reflecting well-maintained conductivity and electrochemical reaction kinetics (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/medium/am4c18772_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Computed Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barriers and electronic structure of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>. (a,d) Na1–Na1 diffusion paths and energy barriers. (b,e) Na2–Na2 diffusion paths and energy barriers. (c,f) Na3–Na3 diffusion paths and energy barriers. (g,h) Simulated pDOS and diagram of energy versus DOS for Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. (i,j) Simulated pDOS and diagram of energy versus DOS for Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/asset/images/large/am4c18772_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c18772&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have developed an Fe-based tunnel-type Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode with high working potential and specific capacity to improve the energy density of ARSIB. It is revealed that the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> redox of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> during sodiation/desodiation promotes a high working potential in contrast to the low potential Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> reaction for traditional Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The doping Sn performs as a hinge to relieve lattice strains and stabilize the tunnel structure. Moreover, the Sn 4d orbital contributing to the conduction band of Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Mn<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>Sn<sub>0.04</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> is beneficial to good electrochemical performances. The full battery, with a NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C anode, gives a high energy density of 80 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>. Additionally, the high stable tunnel-type structure contributes to the long cycle life of up to 1000 cycles as well as good rate performance. The advantages of the presently developed high potential tunnel-type cathode motivate further exploration of energy-dense electrode materials for ARSIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c18772?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c18772</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed experimental section, crystallographic data and powder XRD Rietveld refinement results, SEM images, XRD patterns, CV profiles, Galvanostatic charge–discharge voltage profiles, cycle performances, EIS results, and fitting data of the <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c18772/suppl_file/am4c18772_si_001.pdf">am4c18772_si_001.pdf (1.25 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c18772" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The irreversible momentum of clean energy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Obama, Barack</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An Outlook on Lithium Ion Battery Technology</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium ion batteries as a power source are dominating in portable electronics, penetrating the elec. vehicle market, and on the verge of entering the utility market for grid-energy storage.  Depending on the application, trade-offs among the various performance parameters-energy, power, cycle life, cost, safety, and environmental impact-are often needed, which are linked to severe materials chem. challenges.  The current lithium ion battery technol. is based on insertion-reaction electrodes and org. liq. electrolytes.  With an aim to increase the energy d. or optimize the other performance parameters, new electrode materials based on both insertion reaction and dominantly conversion reaction along with solid electrolytes and lithium metal anode are being intensively pursued.  This article presents an outlook on lithium ion technol. by providing first the current status and then the progress and challenges with the ongoing approaches.  In light of the formidable challenges with some of the approaches, the article finally points out practically viable near-term strategies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-020-19991-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Voltage issue of aqueous rechargeable metal-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhuoxin; Huang, Yan; Huang, Yang; Yang, Qi; Li, Xinliang; Huang, Zhaodong; Zhi, Chunyi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">180-232</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Over the past two decades, a series of aq. rechargeable metal-ion batteries (ARMBs) have been developed, aiming at improving safety, environmental friendliness and cost-efficiency in fields of consumer electronics, elec. vehicles and grid-scale energy storage.  However, the notable gap between ARMBs and their org. counterparts in energy d. directly hinders their practical applications, making it difficult to replace current widely-used org. lithium-ion batteries.  Basically, this huge gap in energy d. originates from cell voltage, as the narrow electrochem. stability window of aq. electrolytes substantially confines the choice of electrode materials.  This review highlights various ARMBs with focuses on their voltage characteristics and strategies that can effectively raise battery voltage.  It begins with the discussion on the fundamental factor that limits the voltage of ARMBs, i.e., electrochem. stability window of aq. electrolytes, which decides the max.-allowed p.d. between cathode and anode.  The following section introduces various ARMB systems and compares their voltage characteristics in midpoint voltage and plateau voltage, in relation to resp. electrode materials.  Subsequently, various strategies paving the way to high-voltage ARMBs are summarized, with corresponding advancements highlighted.  The final section presents potential directions for further improvements and future perspectives of this thriving field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2023.02.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Regulating the Local Chemical Environment in Layered O3-NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 Achieves Practicable Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>56</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">631</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">641</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

F.; Tevar, A.; Sharma, S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we demonstrate Na4Mn9O18 as a sodium intercalation pos. electrode material for an aq. electrolyte energy storage device.  A simple solid-state synthesis route was used to produce this material, which was then tested electrochem. in a 1 M Na2SO4 electrolyte against an activated carbon counter electrode using cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic cycling.  Optimized Na4Mn9O18 was documented as having a specific capacity of 45 mAh/g through a voltage range of 0.5 V, or an equiv. specific capacitance of over 300 F/g.  With the proper neg.:pos. electrode mass ratio, energy storage cells capable of being charged to at least 1.7 V without significant water electrolysis are documented.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanowire Na0.35MnO2 from a hydrothermal method as a cathode material for aqueous asymmetric supercapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, B.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

Due to the nanowire structure the capacitance (157 F/g) is much higher than that of the rod-like Na0.95MnO2 (92 F/g) from solid phase reaction although sodium content is lower.  When it is assembled into an asym. aq. supercapacitor using activated carbon as the counter electrode and aq. 0.5 mol/L Na2SO4 electrolyte soln., the Na0.35MnO2 nanowires show an energy d. of 42.6 W-h/kg at a power d. of 129.8 W/kg based on the total wt. of the two electrode materials, higher than those for the rod-like Na0.95MnO2, with an energy d. of 27.3 W-h/kg at a power d. of 74.8 W/kg, and that of LiMn2O4.  The new material presents excellent cycling behavior even when dissolved oxygen is not removed from the electrolyte soln.  The results hold great promise for practical applications of this cathode material since sodium is much cheaper than lithium and its natural resources are rich.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06018K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An Aqueous Rechargeable Sodium Ion Battery Based on A NaMnO<sub>2</sub>–NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Hybrid System for Stationary Energy Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An aqueous rechargeable sodium ion battery based on a NaMnO2-NaTi2(PO4)3 hybrid system for stationary energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou, Zhiguo; Li, Xiaona; Liang, Jianwen; Zhu, Youngchun; Qian, Yitai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to the costly short-term transients, frequency regulation, and load balancing, the elec. power grid faces an urgent need for large-scale energy storage.  The long durability, high power and energy d., and low cost needed for stationary energy storage posing const. challenges for conventional battery technol. inspire people to explore new kinds of energy storage technologies.  Here, the authors assembled an aq. rechargeable Na ion battery by using NaMnO2 as a cathode material and NaTi2(PO4)3/C composites as anode materials in 2 M CH3COONa aq. electrolyte.  This battery system could work in a wide voltage range from 0.5 V to 1.8 V, giving an energy d. of 30 W h kg-1 (based on the total mass of active materials) and could retain 75% of the initial capacity after 500 cycles at the 5 C rate.  What is more, the earth-abundant precursors, environmental friendliness and inherent safety made this battery system particularly attractive for stationary energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms13370"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Charles, D.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural water and disordered structure promote aqueous sodium-ion energy storage in sodium-birnessite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shan Xiaoqiang; Guo Fenghua; Charles Daniel S; Teng Xiaowei; Lebens-Higgins Zachary; Abdel Razek Sara; Piper Louis F J; Wu Jinpeng; Yang Wanli; Xu Wenqian; Page Katharine L; Neuefeind Joerg C; Feygenson Mikhail; Feygenson Mikhail</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4975</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Birnessite is a low-cost and environmentally friendly layered material for aqueous electrochemical energy storage; however, its storage capacity is poor due to its narrow potential window in aqueous electrolyte and low redox activity.  Herein we report a sodium rich disordered birnessite (Na0.27MnO2) for aqueous sodium-ion electrochemical storage with a much-enhanced capacity and cycling life (83 mAh g(-1) after 5000 cycles in full-cell).  Neutron total scattering and in situ X-ray diffraction measurements show that both structural water and the Na-rich disordered structure contribute to the improved electrochemical performance of current cathode material.  Particularly, the co-deintercalation of the hydrated water and sodium-ion during the high potential charging process results in the shrinkage of interlayer distance and thus stabilizes the layered structure.  Our results provide a genuine insight into how structural disordering and structural water improve sodium-ion storage in a layered electrode and open up an exciting direction for improving aqueous batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202300334"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ahmad, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress in the Emerging Modification Strategies for Layered Oxide Cathodes toward Practicable Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Progress in the Emerging Modification Strategies for Layered Oxide Cathodes toward Practicable Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Bo; Wan, Guanglin; Ahmad, Nazir; Yu, Lai; Ma, Xinyi; Zhang, Genqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2300334</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Low-cost sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been extensively considered as a supplement or even a replacement for successful lithium-ion batteries.  However, the practical application of SIBs is limited by their energy d. and cyclic performance, which are mainly constrained by the cathode side.  Therefore, the development of advanced cathode materials is essential for the practical application of SIBs.  Among the various cathode materials, layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs) are highly promising candidates due to their compact crystal structure, low-cost, ease of prepn., and similarities to successful Li-based LTMOs.  Unfortunately, the bottleneck issues faced by Na-based LTMOs, such as severe phase transitions, sluggish diffusion kinetics, and interface deterioration, pose significant challenges in achieving high-performance cathodes.  In this review, a comprehensive overview and summary of recently reported modification strategies for layered oxides are provided and the structure-function-performance relationship is refined.  A perspective on the outlook and development direction for Na-based LTMOs cathodes is also provided.  This review comprehensively explores the modification strategies of Na-based LTMOs, providing a new horizon for future research on Na-based LTMOs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms4007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pasta, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wessells, C.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Voltage Aqueous Na-Ion Battery Enabled by Inert-Cation-Assisted Water-in-Salt Electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Liwei; Liu, Lilu; Yue, Jinming; Zhang, Qiangqiang; Zhou, Anxing; Borodin, Oleg; Suo, Liumin; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Xu, Kang; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">H2O-in-salt (WiS) electrolytes provide a new pathway to widen the electrochem. window of aq. electrolytes.  However, their formulation strongly depends on the soly. of the chosen salts, imposing a stringent restriction on the no. of possible WiS systems.  This issue becomes more severe for aq. Na-ion batteries (ANIBs) owing to the relatively lower soly. of Na salts compared to its alk. cousins (Li, K, and Cs).  A new class of the inert-cation-assisted WiS (IC-WiS) electrolytes contg. the Et4N+ (TEA+) inert cation is reported.  The Na IC-WiS electrolyte at a superhigh concn. of 31 mol kg-1 exhibits a wide electrochem. window of 3.3 V, suppresses transition metal dissoln. from the cathode, and ensures singular intercalation of Na into both cathode and anode electrodes during cycling, which is often problematic in mixed alkali cation systems such as K-Na and Li-Na.  Owing to these unique advantages of the IC-WiS electrolyte, the NaTiOPO4 anode and Prussian blue analog Na1.88Mn[Fe(CN)6]0.97·1.35H2O cathode can be coupled to construct a full ANIB, delivering an av. voltage of 1.74 V and a high energy d. of 71 Wh kg-1 with a capacity retention of 90% after 200 cycles at 0.25 C and of 76% over 800 cycles at 1C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201905115"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Man, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Conversion Synthesis of Self-Standing Potassium Zinc Hexacyanoferrate Arrays as Cathodes for High-Voltage Flexible Aqueous Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">52</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1905115</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Aqueous Sodium-Ion Battery using a Na3V2(PO4)3 Electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Weixin; Ji, Xiaobo; Zhu, Yirong; Zhu, Hanjun; Li, Fangqian; Chen, Jun; Lu, Fang; Yao, Yinpeng; Banks, Craig. E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">871-876</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHEMRA</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The NASICON-type Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) cathode material is investigated in an aq. sodium-ion battery, which is explored by using a three-electrode system.  The battery behaviors and capacitive properties of this electrode system are critically investigated by using 1 M Li2SO4, Na2SO4, and K2SO4 electrolytes, with an optimal performance found to arise in Na+-based electrolyte, which exhibits a capacitance of 209 Fg-1 at 8.5 C as well as enhanced ion diffusion.  Larger, hydrated Li+ is less able to diffuse into the network of NVP, and the low cond. and mobility leads to near noncapacitive behavior.  In the case of K+-based electrolyte, NVP presents asym. cyclic voltammograms, owing to weak solvation and the high cond. of K+, making the ions more easily able to form elec. double-layer capacitance on the surface or pores of NVP, rather than insert into the network.  The equivalent circuit based on electrochem. impedance spectroscopy result is analyzed to account for the electrochem. insertion behavior of Na+ into NVP, involving ion transfer in electrolyte soln., ion diffusion from the electrolyte to the electrode surface, as well as charge transfer and ion diffusion in the electrode solid.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA09194C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nian, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na 3 V 2 (PO 4) 2 F 3–SWCNT: A High Voltage Cathode for Non-Aqueous and Aqueous Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
